<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Archimedes</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Greek mathematician and physicist</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Archimedes_(disambiguation)" title="Archimedes (disambiguation)">Archimedes (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox biography vcard"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" style="font-size:125%;"><div class="fn" style="display:inline">Archimedes of Syracuse</div></th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-subheader" colspan="2" style="font-size:125%; font-weight:bold;"><div class="nickname" lang="Greek">Ἀρχιμήδης</div></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-image" colspan="2"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Domenico-Fetti_Archimedes_1620.jpg"><img alt="A painting of an older man puzzling over geometric problems" data-file-height="1818" data-file-width="1364" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Domenico-Fetti_Archimedes_1620.jpg/220px-Domenico-Fetti_Archimedes_1620.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Domenico-Fetti_Archimedes_1620.jpg/330px-Domenico-Fetti_Archimedes_1620.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Domenico-Fetti_Archimedes_1620.jpg/440px-Domenico-Fetti_Archimedes_1620.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a><div class="infobox-caption"><i>Archimedes Thoughtful</i><br/>by <a href="/wiki/Domenico_Fetti" title="Domenico Fetti">Domenico Fetti</a> (1620)</div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Born</th><td class="infobox-data"><abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 287<span class="nowrap"> </span>BC</span><br/><div class="birthplace" style="display:inline"><a href="/wiki/Syracuse,_Sicily" title="Syracuse, Sicily">Syracuse, Sicily</a></div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Died</th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="circa">c.</span><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 212<span class="nowrap"> </span>BC</span> (aged approximately 75)<br/><div class="deathplace" style="display:inline">Syracuse, Sicily</div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Known for</th><td class="infobox-data"><div class="mw-collapsible mw-collapsed" style="text-align: center; font-size: 95%;">
<div style="line-height: 1.6em; font-weight: bold; font-size: 100%; text-align: left;"><div>List</div></div>
<ul class="mw-collapsible-content" style="font-size: 105%; margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0; line-height: inherit; text-align: left; list-style: none; margin-left: 0;"><li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0"><a href="/wiki/Archimedes%27_principle" title="Archimedes' principle">Archimedes' principle</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Archimedes%27_screw" title="Archimedes' screw">Archimedes' screw</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Center_of_mass#History" title="Center of mass">Center of gravity</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Statics" title="Statics">Statics</a><br/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluid_statics" title="Fluid statics">Hydrostatics</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Lever" title="Lever">Law of the lever</a><br/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archimedes%27_use_of_infinitesimals" title="Archimedes' use of infinitesimals">Indivisibles</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Neusis_construction" title="Neusis construction">Neuseis constructions</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><br/><a href="/wiki/List_of_things_named_after_Archimedes" title="List of things named after Archimedes">List of other things named after him</a></li></ul>
</div></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><b>Scientific career</b></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Fields</th><td class="infobox-data category"><a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">Mathematics</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">Physics</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">Engineering</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">Astronomy</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Mechanics" title="Mechanics">Mechanics</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Influences</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Eudoxus_of_Cnidus" title="Eudoxus of Cnidus">Eudoxus</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Influenced</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Apollonius_of_Perga" title="Apollonius of Perga">Apollonius</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> <br/><a href="/wiki/Hero_of_Alexandria" title="Hero of Alexandria">Hero</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Pappus_of_Alexandria" title="Pappus of Alexandria">Pappus</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Eutocius_of_Ascalon" title="Eutocius of Ascalon">Eutocius</a></td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Archimedes of Syracuse</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="/ɑːr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ɑːr</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins-3">[3]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc"><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%88%CF%81%CF%87%CE%B9%CE%BC%CE%AE%CE%B4%CE%B7%CF%82#Ancient_Greek" title="wikt:Ἀρχιμήδης">Ἀρχιμήδης</a></span></span>; <small><a href="/wiki/Doric_Greek" title="Doric Greek">Doric Greek</a>: </small><span class="IPA" lang="grc-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Greek" title="Help:IPA/Greek">[ar.kʰi.mɛː.dɛ̂ːs]</a></span>; <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 287</span> – c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 212<span class="nowrap"> </span>BC</span>) was a <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greek</a> <a href="/wiki/Greek_mathematics" title="Greek mathematics">mathematician</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physicist" title="Physicist">physicist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Engineer" title="Engineer">engineer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Astronomer" title="Astronomer">astronomer</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Invention" title="Invention">inventor</a> from the ancient city of <a href="/wiki/Syracuse,_Sicily" title="Syracuse, Sicily">Syracuse</a> in <a href="/wiki/History_of_Greek_and_Hellenistic_Sicily" title="History of Greek and Hellenistic Sicily">Sicily</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Although few details of his life are known, he is regarded as one of the leading scientists in <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a>. Considered to be the greatest mathematician of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">ancient history</a>, and one of the greatest of all time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LitList_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-LitList-5">[5]</a></sup> Archimedes anticipated modern <a href="/wiki/Calculus" title="Calculus">calculus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_analysis" title="Mathematical analysis">analysis</a> by applying the concept of the <a href="/wiki/Cavalieri%27s_principle" title="Cavalieri's principle">infinitely small</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Method_of_exhaustion" title="Method of exhaustion">method of exhaustion</a> to derive and rigorously prove a range of <a href="/wiki/Geometry" title="Geometry">geometrical</a> <a href="/wiki/Theorem" title="Theorem">theorems</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-:9-7">[7]</a></sup> including: the <a href="/wiki/Area_of_a_circle" title="Area of a circle">area of a circle</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Surface_area" title="Surface area">surface area</a> and <a href="/wiki/Volume" title="Volume">volume</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Sphere" title="Sphere">sphere</a>; area of an <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">ellipse</a>; the area under a <a href="/wiki/Parabola" title="Parabola">parabola</a>; the volume of a segment of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paraboloid_of_revolution" title="Paraboloid of revolution">paraboloid of revolution</a>; the volume of a segment of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperboloid_of_revolution" title="Hyperboloid of revolution">hyperboloid of revolution</a>; and the area of a <a href="/wiki/Spiral" title="Spiral">spiral</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Archimedes' other mathematical achievements include deriving an <a href="/wiki/Approximations_of_%CF%80" title="Approximations of π">approximation of pi</a>; defining and investigating the <a href="/wiki/Archimedean_spiral" title="Archimedean spiral">spiral that now bears his name</a>; and devising a system using <a href="/wiki/Exponentiation" title="Exponentiation">exponentiation</a> for expressing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Very_large_numbers" title="Very large numbers">very large numbers</a>. He was also one of the first to <a href="/wiki/Applied_mathematics" title="Applied mathematics">apply mathematics</a> to <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physical phenomena</a>, founding <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatics" title="Hydrostatics">hydrostatics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Statics" title="Statics">statics</a>. Archimedes' achievements in this area include a proof of the principle of the <a href="/wiki/Lever" title="Lever">lever</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> the widespread use of the concept of <a href="/wiki/Center_of_mass#History" title="Center of mass">center of gravity</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> and the enunciation of the <a href="/wiki/Buoyancy#Archimedes'_principle" title="Buoyancy">law of buoyancy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-:7-12">[12]</a></sup> He is also credited with designing innovative <a href="/wiki/Machine" title="Machine">machines</a>, such as his <a href="/wiki/Archimedes%27_screw" title="Archimedes' screw">screw pump</a>, <a href="/wiki/Block_and_tackle" title="Block and tackle">compound pulleys</a>, and defensive war machines to protect his native <a href="/wiki/Syracuse,_Sicily" title="Syracuse, Sicily">Syracuse</a> from invasion.
</p><p>Archimedes died during the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Syracuse_(213%E2%80%93212_BC)" title="Siege of Syracuse (213–212 BC)">siege of Syracuse</a>, when he was killed by a Roman soldier despite orders that he should not be harmed. <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> describes visiting Archimedes' tomb, which was surmounted by a <a href="/wiki/Sphere" title="Sphere">sphere</a> and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cylinder_(geometry)" title="Cylinder (geometry)">cylinder</a>, which Archimedes had requested be placed on his tomb to represent his mathematical discoveries.
</p><p>Unlike his inventions, Archimedes' mathematical writings were little known in antiquity. Mathematicians from <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> read and quoted him, but the first comprehensive compilation was not made until <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 530<span class="nowrap"> </span>AD</span> by <a href="/wiki/Isidore_of_Miletus" title="Isidore of Miletus">Isidore of Miletus</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine" title="Byzantine">Byzantine</a> <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, while commentaries on the works of Archimedes by <a href="/wiki/Eutocius_of_Ascalon" title="Eutocius of Ascalon">Eutocius</a> in the 6th century opened them to wider readership for the first time. The relatively few copies of Archimedes' written work that survived through the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> were an influential source of ideas for scientists during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_science_in_the_Renaissance" title="History of science in the Renaissance">Renaissance</a> and again <a href="/wiki/Scientific_Revolution" title="Scientific Revolution">in the 17th century</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> while the discovery in 1906 of previously lost works by Archimedes in the <a href="/wiki/Archimedes_Palimpsest" title="Archimedes Palimpsest">Archimedes Palimpsest</a> has provided new insights into how he obtained mathematical results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biography">Biography</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Death_of_Archimedes_(1815)_by_Thomas_Degeorge.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1084" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Death_of_Archimedes_%281815%29_by_Thomas_Degeorge.png/220px-Death_of_Archimedes_%281815%29_by_Thomas_Degeorge.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Death_of_Archimedes_%281815%29_by_Thomas_Degeorge.png/330px-Death_of_Archimedes_%281815%29_by_Thomas_Degeorge.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Death_of_Archimedes_%281815%29_by_Thomas_Degeorge.png/440px-Death_of_Archimedes_%281815%29_by_Thomas_Degeorge.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Death_of_Archimedes_(1815)_by_Thomas_Degeorge.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Death of Archimedes</i> (1815) by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Degeorge" title="Thomas Degeorge">Thomas Degeorge</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Archimedes was born c. 287 BC in the seaport city of <a href="/wiki/Syracuse,_Sicily" title="Syracuse, Sicily">Syracuse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>, at that time a self-governing colony in <a href="/wiki/Magna_Graecia" title="Magna Graecia">Magna Graecia</a>. The date of birth is based on a statement by the Byzantine Greek historian <a href="/wiki/John_Tzetzes" title="John Tzetzes">John Tzetzes</a> that Archimedes lived for 75 years before his death in 212 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-9">[9]</a></sup> In the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sand_Reckoner" title="The Sand Reckoner">Sand-Reckoner</a></i>, Archimedes gives his father's name as Phidias, an astronomer about whom nothing else is known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-20">[20]</a></sup> A biography of Archimedes was written by his friend Heracleides, but this work has been lost, leaving the details of his life obscure. It is unknown, for instance, whether he ever married or had children, or if he ever visited <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, Egypt, during his youth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-21">[21]</a></sup> From his surviving written works, it is clear that he maintained collegiate relations with scholars based there, including his friend <a href="/wiki/Conon_of_Samos" title="Conon of Samos">Conon of Samos</a> and the head librarian <a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes" title="Eratosthenes">Eratosthenes of Cyrene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[a]</a></sup>
</p><p>The standard versions of Archimedes' life were written long after his death by Greek and Roman historians. The earliest reference to Archimedes occurs in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Histories_(Polybius)" title="The Histories (Polybius)">The Histories</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Polybius" title="Polybius">Polybius</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 200–118 BC), written about 70 years after his death. It sheds little light on Archimedes as a person, and focuses on the war machines that he is said to have built in order to defend the city from the Romans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-death_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-death-23">[22]</a></sup> Polybius remarks how, during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Punic_War" title="Second Punic War">Second Punic War</a>, Syracuse switched allegiances from <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Rome</a> to <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a>, resulting in a military campaign to take the city under the command of <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Claudius_Marcellus" title="Marcus Claudius Marcellus">Marcus Claudius Marcellus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Appius_Claudius_Pulcher_(consul_212_BC)" title="Appius Claudius Pulcher (consul 212 BC)">Appius Claudius Pulcher</a>, which lasted from 213 to 212 BC. He notes that the Romans underestimated Syracuse's defenses, and mentions several machines Archimedes designed, including improved catapults, cranelike machines that could be swung around in an arc, and stone-throwers. Although the Romans ultimately captured the city, they suffered considerable losses due to Archimedes' inventiveness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cicero_Discovering_the_Tomb_of_Archimedes_by_Benjamin_West.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4081" data-file-width="5937" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Cicero_Discovering_the_Tomb_of_Archimedes_by_Benjamin_West.jpeg/220px-Cicero_Discovering_the_Tomb_of_Archimedes_by_Benjamin_West.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Cicero_Discovering_the_Tomb_of_Archimedes_by_Benjamin_West.jpeg/330px-Cicero_Discovering_the_Tomb_of_Archimedes_by_Benjamin_West.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Cicero_Discovering_the_Tomb_of_Archimedes_by_Benjamin_West.jpeg/440px-Cicero_Discovering_the_Tomb_of_Archimedes_by_Benjamin_West.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cicero_Discovering_the_Tomb_of_Archimedes_by_Benjamin_West.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> Discovering the Tomb of Archimedes</i> (1805) by <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_West" title="Benjamin West">Benjamin West</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> (106–43 BC) mentions Archimedes in some of his works. While serving as a <a href="/wiki/Quaestor" title="Quaestor">quaestor</a> in Sicily, Cicero found what was presumed to be Archimedes' tomb near the Agrigentine gate in Syracuse, in a neglected condition and overgrown with bushes. Cicero had the tomb cleaned up and was able to see the carving and read some of the verses that had been added as an inscription. The tomb carried a sculpture illustrating Archimedes' <a href="/wiki/On_the_Sphere_and_Cylinder" title="On the Sphere and Cylinder">favorite mathematical proof</a>, that the volume and surface area of the sphere are two-thirds that of the cylinder including its bases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> He also mentions that Marcellus brought to Rome two planetariums Archimedes built.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-27">[26]</a></sup> The Roman historian <a href="/wiki/Livy" title="Livy">Livy</a> (59 BC–17 AD) retells Polybius' story of the capture of Syracuse and Archimedes' role in it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-death_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-death-23">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> (45–119 AD) wrote in his <i><a href="/wiki/Parallel_Lives" title="Parallel Lives">Parallel Lives</a></i> that Archimedes was related to King <a href="/wiki/Hiero_II_of_Syracuse" title="Hiero II of Syracuse">Hiero II</a>, the ruler of Syracuse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup> He also provides at least two accounts on how Archimedes died after the city was taken. According to the most popular account, Archimedes was contemplating a mathematical diagram when the city was captured. A Roman soldier commanded him to come and meet Marcellus, but he declined, saying that he had to finish working on the problem. This enraged the soldier, who killed Archimedes with his sword. Another story has Archimedes carrying mathematical instruments before being killed because a soldier thought they were valuable items. Marcellus was reportedly angered by Archimedes' death, as he considered him a valuable scientific asset (he called Archimedes "a geometrical <a href="/wiki/Hecatoncheires" title="Hecatoncheires">Briareus</a>") and had ordered that he should not be harmed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-29">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>The last words attributed to Archimedes are "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Do_not_disturb_my_circles" title="Do not disturb my circles">Do not disturb my circles</a>" (<a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, "<i>Noli turbare circulos meos</i>"; <a href="/wiki/Katharevousa" title="Katharevousa">Katharevousa Greek</a>, "μὴ μου τοὺς κύκλους τάραττε"), a reference to the mathematical drawing that he was supposedly studying when disturbed by the Roman soldier. There is no reliable evidence that Archimedes uttered these words and they do not appear in Plutarch's account. A similar quotation is found in the work of <a href="/wiki/Valerius_Maximus" title="Valerius Maximus">Valerius Maximus</a> (fl. 30 AD), who wrote in <i>Memorable Doings and Sayings</i>, "<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">... sed protecto manibus puluere 'noli' inquit, 'obsecro, istum disturbare'</i></span>" ("... but protecting the dust with his hands, said 'I beg of you, do not disturb this<span style="padding-right:.15em;">'</span>").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-death_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-death-23">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Discoveries_and_inventions">Discoveries and inventions</span></h2>
<h3><span id="Archimedes.27_principle"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Archimedes'_principle">Archimedes' principle</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Archimedes%27_principle" title="Archimedes' principle">Archimedes' principle</a></div>

<p>The most widely known anecdote about Archimedes tells of how he invented a method for determining the volume of an object with an irregular shape. According to <a href="/wiki/Vitruvius" title="Vitruvius">Vitruvius</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Votive_crown" title="Votive crown">votive crown</a> for a temple had been made for <a href="/wiki/Hiero_II_of_Syracuse" title="Hiero II of Syracuse">King Hiero II of Syracuse</a>, who had supplied the pure gold to be used; Archimedes was asked to determine whether some silver had been substituted by the dishonest goldsmith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vitruvius_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-vitruvius-31">[30]</a></sup> Archimedes had to solve the problem without damaging the crown, so he could not melt it down into a regularly shaped body in order to calculate its <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a>.
</p><p>In Vitruvius' account, Archimedes noticed while taking a bath that the level of the water in the tub rose as he got in, and realized that this effect could be used to determine the crown's <a href="/wiki/Volume" title="Volume">volume</a>. For practical purposes water is incompressible,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup> so the submerged crown would displace an amount of water equal to its own volume. By dividing the mass of the crown by the volume of water displaced, the density of the crown could be obtained. This density would be lower than that of gold if cheaper and less dense metals had been added. Archimedes then took to the streets naked, so excited by his discovery that he had forgotten to dress, crying "<a href="/wiki/Eureka_(word)" title="Eureka (word)">Eureka</a>!" (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">"εὕρηκα</span>, <i>heúrēka</i>!, <abbr title="literal translation">lit.</abbr><span style="white-space: nowrap;"> </span>'I have found [it]!').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vitruvius_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-vitruvius-31">[30]</a></sup> The test on the crown was conducted successfully, proving that silver had indeed been mixed in.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>The story of the golden crown does not appear anywhere in Archimedes' known works. The practicality of the method it describes has been called into question due to the extreme accuracy that would be required while measuring the <a href="/wiki/Displacement_(fluid)" title="Displacement (fluid)">water displacement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inaccuracy_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-inaccuracy-34">[33]</a></sup> Archimedes may have instead sought a solution that applied the principle known in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluid_statics" title="Fluid statics">hydrostatics</a> as <a href="/wiki/Archimedes%27_principle" title="Archimedes' principle">Archimedes' principle</a>, which he describes in his treatise <i><a href="/wiki/On_Floating_Bodies" title="On Floating Bodies">On Floating Bodies</a></i>. This principle states that a body immersed in a fluid experiences a <a href="/wiki/Buoyancy" title="Buoyancy">buoyant force</a> equal to the weight of the fluid it displaces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> Using this principle, it would have been possible to compare the density of the crown to that of pure gold by balancing the crown on a scale with a pure gold reference sample of the same weight, then immersing the apparatus in water. The difference in density between the two samples would cause the scale to tip accordingly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-:7-12">[12]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a>, who in 1586 invented a hydrostatic balance for weighing metals in air and water inspired by the work of Archimedes, considered it "probable that this method is the same that Archimedes followed, since, besides being very accurate, it is based on demonstrations found by Archimedes himself."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-galileo_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-galileo-37">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Archimedes.27_screw"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Archimedes'_screw">Archimedes' screw</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Archimedes%27_screw" title="Archimedes' screw">Archimedes' screw</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Archimedes-screw_one-screw-threads_with-ball_3D-view_animated_small.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="365" data-file-width="505" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Archimedes-screw_one-screw-threads_with-ball_3D-view_animated_small.gif/220px-Archimedes-screw_one-screw-threads_with-ball_3D-view_animated_small.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Archimedes-screw_one-screw-threads_with-ball_3D-view_animated_small.gif/330px-Archimedes-screw_one-screw-threads_with-ball_3D-view_animated_small.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Archimedes-screw_one-screw-threads_with-ball_3D-view_animated_small.gif/440px-Archimedes-screw_one-screw-threads_with-ball_3D-view_animated_small.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Archimedes-screw_one-screw-threads_with-ball_3D-view_animated_small.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Archimedes%27_screw" title="Archimedes' screw">Archimedes' screw</a> can raise water efficiently.</div></div></div>
<p>A large part of Archimedes' work in engineering probably arose from fulfilling the needs of his home city of <a href="/wiki/Syracuse,_Sicily" title="Syracuse, Sicily">Syracuse</a>. The Greek writer <a href="/wiki/Athenaeus" title="Athenaeus">Athenaeus of Naucratis</a> described how King Hiero II commissioned Archimedes to design a huge ship, the <i><a href="/wiki/Syracusia" title="Syracusia">Syracusia</a></i>, which could be used for luxury travel, carrying supplies, and as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naval_warship" title="Naval warship">naval warship</a>. The <i>Syracusia</i> is said to have been the largest ship built in <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> According to Athenaeus, it was capable of carrying 600 people and included garden decorations, a <a href="/wiki/Gymnasium_(ancient_Greece)" title="Gymnasium (ancient Greece)">gymnasium</a> and a temple dedicated to the goddess <a href="/wiki/Aphrodite" title="Aphrodite">Aphrodite</a> among its facilities. Since a ship of this size would leak a considerable amount of water through the hull, <a href="/wiki/Archimedes%27_screw" title="Archimedes' screw">Archimedes' screw</a> was purportedly developed in order to remove the bilge water. Archimedes' machine was a device with a revolving screw-shaped blade inside a cylinder. It was turned by hand, and could also be used to transfer water from a <span class="nowrap">low-lying</span> body of water into irrigation canals. Archimedes' screw is still in use today for pumping liquids and granulated solids such as coal and grain. Described in Roman times by Vitruvius, Archimedes' screw may have been an improvement on a screw pump that was used to irrigate the <a href="/wiki/Hanging_Gardens_of_Babylon" title="Hanging Gardens of Babylon">Hanging Gardens of Babylon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> The world's first seagoing <a href="/wiki/Steamboat" title="Steamboat">steamship</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Propeller" title="Propeller">screw propeller</a> was the <a href="/wiki/SS_Archimedes" title="SS Archimedes">SS <i>Archimedes</i></a>, which was launched in 1839 and named in honor of Archimedes and his work on the screw.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Claw_of_Archimedes">Claw of Archimedes</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Claw_of_Archimedes" title="Claw of Archimedes">Claw of Archimedes</a> is a weapon that he is said to have designed in order to defend the city of Syracuse. Also known as "the ship shaker", the claw consisted of a crane-like arm from which a large metal <a href="/wiki/Grappling_hook" title="Grappling hook">grappling hook</a> was suspended. When the claw was dropped onto an attacking ship the arm would swing upwards, lifting the ship out of the water and possibly sinking it. There have been modern experiments to test the feasibility of the claw, and in 2005 a television documentary entitled <i>Superweapons of the Ancient World</i> built a version of the claw and concluded that it was a workable device.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heat_ray">Heat ray</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Archimedes_Heat_Ray_conceptual_diagram.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="358" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="263" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Archimedes_Heat_Ray_conceptual_diagram.svg/220px-Archimedes_Heat_Ray_conceptual_diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Archimedes_Heat_Ray_conceptual_diagram.svg/330px-Archimedes_Heat_Ray_conceptual_diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Archimedes_Heat_Ray_conceptual_diagram.svg/440px-Archimedes_Heat_Ray_conceptual_diagram.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Archimedes_Heat_Ray_conceptual_diagram.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Archimedes may have used mirrors acting collectively as a <a href="/wiki/Parabolic_reflector" title="Parabolic reflector">parabolic reflector</a> to burn ships attacking <a href="/wiki/Syracuse,_Sicily" title="Syracuse, Sicily">Syracuse</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Archimedes may have used mirrors acting collectively as a <a href="/wiki/Parabolic_reflector" title="Parabolic reflector">parabolic reflector</a> to burn ships attacking Syracuse. The 2nd-century author <a href="/wiki/Lucian" title="Lucian">Lucian</a> wrote that during the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Syracuse_(213%E2%80%93212_BC)" title="Siege of Syracuse (213–212 BC)">siege of Syracuse</a> (c. 214–212 BC), Archimedes destroyed enemy ships with fire. Centuries later, <a href="/wiki/Anthemius_of_Tralles" title="Anthemius of Tralles">Anthemius of Tralles</a> mentions <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burning-glass" title="Burning-glass">burning-glasses</a> as Archimedes' weapon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> The device, sometimes called the "Archimedes heat ray", was used to focus sunlight onto approaching ships, causing them to catch fire. In the modern era, similar devices have been constructed and may be referred to as a <a href="/wiki/Heliostat" title="Heliostat">heliostat</a> or <a href="/wiki/Solar_furnace" title="Solar furnace">solar furnace</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>This purported weapon has been the subject of an ongoing debate about its credibility since the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a> rejected it as false, while modern researchers have attempted to recreate the effect using only the means that would have been available to Archimedes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup> It has been suggested that a large array of highly polished <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a> or <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> shields acting as mirrors could have been employed to focus sunlight onto a ship.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lever">Lever</span></h3>
<p>While Archimedes did not invent the <a href="/wiki/Lever" title="Lever">lever</a>, he gave a mathematical proof of the principle involved in his work <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Equilibrium_of_Planes" title="On the Equilibrium of Planes">On the Equilibrium of Planes</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> Earlier descriptions of the lever are found in the <a href="/wiki/Peripatetic_school" title="Peripatetic school">Peripatetic school</a> of the followers of <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, and are sometimes attributed to <a href="/wiki/Archytas" title="Archytas">Archytas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lever_rorres_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-lever_rorres-48">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lever_clagett_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-lever_clagett-49">[48]</a></sup> There are several, often conflicting, reports regarding Archimedes' feats using the lever to lift very heavy objects. Plutarch describes how Archimedes designed <a href="/wiki/Block_and_tackle" title="Block and tackle">block-and-tackle</a> <a href="/wiki/Pulley" title="Pulley">pulley</a> systems, allowing sailors to use the principle of <a href="/wiki/Lever" title="Lever">leverage</a> to lift objects that would otherwise have been too heavy to move.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Pappus_of_Alexandria" title="Pappus of Alexandria">Pappus of Alexandria</a>, Archimedes' work on levers caused him to remark: "Give me a place to stand on, and I will move the Earth" (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">δῶς μοι πᾶ στῶ καὶ τὰν γᾶν κινάσω</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Olympiodorus_the_Younger" title="Olympiodorus the Younger">Olympiodorus</a> later attributed the same boast to Archimedes' invention of the <i>baroulkos</i>, a kind of <a href="/wiki/Windlass" title="Windlass">windlass</a>, rather than the lever.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Archimedes has also been credited with improving the power and accuracy of the <a href="/wiki/Catapult" title="Catapult">catapult</a>, and with inventing the <a href="/wiki/Odometer" title="Odometer">odometer</a> during the <a href="/wiki/First_Punic_War" title="First Punic War">First Punic War</a>. The odometer was described as a cart with a gear mechanism that dropped a ball into a container after each mile traveled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomical_instruments">Astronomical instruments</span></h3>
<p>Archimedes discusses astronomical measurements of the Earth, Sun, and Moon, as well as <a href="/wiki/Aristarchus_of_Samos" title="Aristarchus of Samos">Aristarchus</a>' heliocentric model of the universe, in the <i>Sand-Reckoner</i>. Despite a lack of trigonometry and a table of chords, Archimedes describes the procedure and instrument used to make observations (a straight rod with pegs or grooves),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> applies correction factors to these measurements, and finally gives the result in the form of upper and lower bounds to account for observational error.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-20">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>, quoting Hipparchus, also references Archimedes' solstice observations in the <i>Almagest</i>. This would make Archimedes the first known Greek to have recorded multiple solstice dates and times in successive years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cicero mentions Archimedes briefly in his <a href="/wiki/Dialogue" title="Dialogue">dialogue</a> <i><a href="/wiki/De_re_publica" title="De re publica">De re publica</a></i>, which portrays a fictional conversation taking place in 129 BC. After the capture of Syracuse c. 212 BC, General <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Claudius_Marcellus" title="Marcus Claudius Marcellus">Marcus Claudius Marcellus</a> is said to have taken back to Rome two mechanisms, constructed by Archimedes and used as aids in astronomy, that showed the motion of the Sun, Moon and five planets. Cicero mentions similar mechanisms designed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thales" title="Thales">Thales of Miletus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eudoxus_of_Cnidus" title="Eudoxus of Cnidus">Eudoxus of Cnidus</a>. The dialogue says that Marcellus kept one of the devices as his only personal loot from Syracuse, and donated the other to the Temple of Virtue in Rome. Marcellus' mechanism was demonstrated, according to Cicero, by <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Sulpicius_Gallus" title="Gaius Sulpicius Gallus">Gaius Sulpicius Gallus</a> to <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Furius_Philus" title="Lucius Furius Philus">Lucius Furius Philus</a>, who described it thus:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1109925960">.mw-parser-output .verse_translation .translated{padding-left:2em}@media only screen and (max-width:43.75em){.mw-parser-output .verse_translation.wrap_when_small td{display:block;padding-left:0}.mw-parser-output .verse_translation.wrap_when_small .translated{padding-left:0}}</style>
<table class="verse_translation wrap_when_small" role="presentation" style="margin-left:1em;">
<tbody><tr style="vertical-align: top;">
<td><div class="poem" lang="" style="font-style:italic;text-align:left">
<p><i>Hanc sphaeram Gallus cum moveret, fiebat ut soli luna totidem conversionibus in aere illo quot diebus in ipso caelo succederet, ex quo et in caelo sphaera solis fieret eadem illa defectio, et incideret luna tum in eam metam quae esset umbra terrae, cum sol e regione.</i>
</p>
</div>
</td>
<td class="translated"><div class="poem">
<p>When Gallus moved the globe, it happened that the Moon followed the Sun by as many turns on that bronze contrivance as in the sky itself, from which also in the sky the Sun's globe became to have that same eclipse, and the Moon came then to that position which was its shadow on the Earth when the Sun was in line.
</p>
</div>
</td></tr>
<tr style="display: none;">
<td>
</td>
<td style="padding-left: 2em;">
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>This is a description of a <a href="/wiki/Planetarium" title="Planetarium">planetarium</a> or <a href="/wiki/Orrery" title="Orrery">orrery</a>. <a href="/wiki/Pappus_of_Alexandria" title="Pappus of Alexandria">Pappus of Alexandria</a> stated that Archimedes had written a manuscript (now lost) on the construction of these mechanisms entitled <i>On Sphere-Making</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> Modern research in this area has been focused on the <a href="/wiki/Antikythera_mechanism" title="Antikythera mechanism">Antikythera mechanism</a>, another device built <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 100</span> BC that was probably designed for the same purpose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup> Constructing mechanisms of this kind would have required a sophisticated knowledge of <a href="/wiki/Differential_(mechanical_device)" title="Differential (mechanical device)">differential gearing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> This was once thought to have been beyond the range of the technology available in ancient times, but the discovery of the Antikythera mechanism in 1902 has confirmed that devices of this kind were known to the ancient Greeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematics">Mathematics</span></h2>
<p>While he is often regarded as a designer of mechanical devices, Archimedes also made contributions to the field of <a href="/wiki/Greek_mathematics" title="Greek mathematics">mathematics</a>. <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> wrote that Archimedes "placed his whole affection and ambition in those purer speculations where there can be no reference to the vulgar needs of life",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-29">[28]</a></sup> though some scholars believe this may be a mischaracterization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Method_of_exhaustion">Method of exhaustion</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PiArchimede4.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="251" data-file-width="420" decoding="async" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/PiArchimede4.svg/220px-PiArchimede4.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/PiArchimede4.svg/330px-PiArchimede4.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/PiArchimede4.svg/440px-PiArchimede4.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PiArchimede4.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Archimedes calculates the side of the 12-gon from that of the <a href="/wiki/Hexagon" title="Hexagon">hexagon</a> and for each subsequent doubling of the sides of the regular polygon.</div></div></div>
<p>Archimedes was able to use <a href="/wiki/Cavalieri%27s_principle" title="Cavalieri's principle">indivisibles</a> (a precursor to <a href="/wiki/Infinitesimal" title="Infinitesimal">infinitesimals</a>) in a way that is similar to modern <a href="/wiki/Integral" title="Integral">integral calculus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-6">[6]</a></sup> Through proof by contradiction (<i><a href="/wiki/Reductio_ad_absurdum" title="Reductio ad absurdum">reductio ad absurdum</a></i>), he could give answers to problems to an arbitrary degree of accuracy, while specifying the limits within which the answer lay. This technique is known as the <a href="/wiki/Method_of_exhaustion" title="Method of exhaustion">method of exhaustion</a>, and he employed it to approximate the areas of figures and the value of <a href="/wiki/Pi" title="Pi">π</a>.
</p><p>In <i><a href="/wiki/Measurement_of_a_Circle" title="Measurement of a Circle">Measurement of a Circle</a></i>, he did this by drawing a larger <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regular_hexagon" title="Regular hexagon">regular hexagon</a> outside a <a href="/wiki/Circle" title="Circle">circle</a> then a smaller regular hexagon inside the circle, and progressively doubling the number of sides of each <a href="/wiki/Regular_polygon" title="Regular polygon">regular polygon</a>, calculating the length of a side of each polygon at each step. As the number of sides increases, it becomes a more accurate approximation of a circle. After four such steps, when the polygons had 96 sides each, he was able to determine that the value of π lay between 3<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1050945101">.mw-parser-output .sfrac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .sfrac.tion,.mw-parser-output .sfrac .tion{display:inline-block;vertical-align:-0.5em;font-size:85%;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .sfrac .num,.mw-parser-output .sfrac .den{display:block;line-height:1em;margin:0 0.1em}.mw-parser-output .sfrac .den{border-top:1px solid}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">7</span></span> (approx. 3.1429) and 3<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">10</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">71</span></span> (approx. 3.1408), consistent with its actual value of approximately 3.1416.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> He also proved that the <a href="/wiki/Area_of_a_circle" title="Area of a circle">area of a circle</a> was equal to π multiplied by the <a href="/wiki/Square" title="Square">square</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Radius" title="Radius">radius</a> of the circle (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\textstyle \pi r^{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
<msup>
<mi>r</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\textstyle \pi r^{2}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\textstyle \pi r^{2}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/327b0b9304eb51864900a5d79bad82d03b95493c" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:3.435ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>). 
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Archimedean_property">Archimedean property</span></h4>
<p>In <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Sphere_and_Cylinder" title="On the Sphere and Cylinder">On the Sphere and Cylinder</a></i>, Archimedes postulates that any magnitude when added to itself enough times will exceed any given magnitude. Today this is known as the <a href="/wiki/Archimedean_property" title="Archimedean property">Archimedean property</a> of real numbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>Archimedes gives the value of the <a href="/wiki/Square_root" title="Square root">square root</a> of 3 as lying between <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">265</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">153</span></span> (approximately 1.7320261) and <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1351</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">780</span></span> (approximately 1.7320512) in <i>Measurement of a Circle</i>. The actual value is approximately 1.7320508, making this a very accurate estimate. He introduced this result without offering any explanation of how he had obtained it. This aspect of the work of Archimedes caused <a href="/wiki/John_Wallis" title="John Wallis">John Wallis</a> to remark that he was: "as it were of set purpose to have covered up the traces of his investigation as if he had grudged posterity the secret of his method of inquiry while he wished to extort from them assent to his results."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup> It is possible that he used an <a href="/wiki/Iteration" title="Iteration">iterative</a> procedure to calculate these values.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_infinite_series">The infinite series</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Parabolic_segment_and_inscribed_triangle.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="243" decoding="async" height="296" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Parabolic_segment_and_inscribed_triangle.svg/180px-Parabolic_segment_and_inscribed_triangle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Parabolic_segment_and_inscribed_triangle.svg/270px-Parabolic_segment_and_inscribed_triangle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Parabolic_segment_and_inscribed_triangle.svg/360px-Parabolic_segment_and_inscribed_triangle.svg.png 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Parabolic_segment_and_inscribed_triangle.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A proof that the area of the <a href="/wiki/Parabola" title="Parabola">parabolic</a> segment in the upper figure is equal to 4/3 that of the inscribed triangle in the lower figure from <i><a href="/wiki/Quadrature_of_the_Parabola" title="Quadrature of the Parabola">Quadrature of the Parabola</a>.</i></div></div></div>
<p>In <i><a href="/wiki/Quadrature_of_the_Parabola" title="Quadrature of the Parabola">Quadrature of the Parabola</a></i>, Archimedes proved that the area enclosed by a <a href="/wiki/Parabola" title="Parabola">parabola</a> and a straight line is <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">4</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">3</span></span> times the area of a corresponding inscribed <a href="/wiki/Triangle" title="Triangle">triangle</a> as shown in the figure at right. He expressed the solution to the problem as an <a href="/wiki/Series_(mathematics)#History_of_the_theory_of_infinite_series" title="Series (mathematics)">infinite</a> <a href="/wiki/Geometric_series" title="Geometric series">geometric series</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Geometric_series#Common_ratio_r" title="Geometric series">common ratio</a> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">4</span></span>:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \sum _{n=0}^{\infty }4^{-n}=1+4^{-1}+4^{-2}+4^{-3}+\cdots ={4 \over 3}.\;}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
