</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\sum _{n=0}^{\infty }4^{-n}=1+4^{-1}+4^{-2}+4^{-3}+\cdots ={4 \over 3}.\;" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a27b8944909dc6de31998c85104a4dfca51187cc" style="vertical-align: -3.005ex; width:42.622ex; height:6.843ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>If the first term in this series is the area of the triangle, then the second is the sum of the areas of two triangles whose bases are the two smaller <a href="/wiki/Secant_line" title="Secant line">secant lines</a>, and whose third vertex is where the line that is parallel to the parabola's axis and that passes through the midpoint of the base intersects the parabola, and so on. This proof uses a variation of the series <span class="nowrap"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1/4_%2B_1/16_%2B_1/64_%2B_1/256_%2B_%C2%B7_%C2%B7_%C2%B7" title="1/4 + 1/16 + 1/64 + 1/256 + · · ·">1/4 + 1/16 + 1/64 + 1/256 + · · ·</a></span> which sums to <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">3</span></span>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Myriad_of_myriads">Myriad of myriads</span></h3><p>
In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sand_Reckoner" title="The Sand Reckoner">The Sand Reckoner</a></i>, Archimedes set out to calculate the number of grains of sand that the universe could contain. In doing so, he challenged the notion that the number of grains of sand was too large to be counted. He wrote:</p><blockquote><p>There are some, King Gelo (Gelo II, son of Hiero II), who think that the number of the sand is infinite in multitude; and I mean by the sand not only that which exists about Syracuse and the rest of Sicily but also that which is found in every region whether inhabited or uninhabited.</p></blockquote><p>To solve the problem, Archimedes devised a system of counting based on the <a href="/wiki/Myriad" title="Myriad">myriad</a>. The word itself derives from the Greek <span title="Ancient Greek-language text"><span lang="grc">μυριάς</span></span>, <span title="Ancient Greek-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">murias</i></span>, for the number 10,000. He proposed a number system using powers of a myriad of myriads (100 million, i.e., 10,000 x 10,000) and concluded that the number of grains of sand required to fill the universe would be 8 <a href="/wiki/Names_of_large_numbers" title="Names of large numbers">vigintillion</a>, or 8<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7001630000000000000♠"></span>63</span></sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup>
</p><h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writings">Writings</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Archimedes_%E2%80%93_Opere,_1615_%E2%80%93_BEIC_9741168.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="1323" decoding="async" height="287" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Archimedes_%E2%80%93_Opere%2C_1615_%E2%80%93_BEIC_9741168.jpg/180px-Archimedes_%E2%80%93_Opere%2C_1615_%E2%80%93_BEIC_9741168.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Archimedes_%E2%80%93_Opere%2C_1615_%E2%80%93_BEIC_9741168.jpg/270px-Archimedes_%E2%80%93_Opere%2C_1615_%E2%80%93_BEIC_9741168.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Archimedes_%E2%80%93_Opere%2C_1615_%E2%80%93_BEIC_9741168.jpg/360px-Archimedes_%E2%80%93_Opere%2C_1615_%E2%80%93_BEIC_9741168.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Archimedes_%E2%80%93_Opere,_1615_%E2%80%93_BEIC_9741168.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Frontpage of Archimedes' <i>Opera</i>, in Greek and Latin, edited by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_Rivault_de_Flurence" title="David Rivault de Flurence">David Rivault</a> (1615).</div></div></div>
<p>The works of Archimedes were written in <a href="/wiki/Doric_Greek" title="Doric Greek">Doric Greek</a>, the dialect of ancient Syracuse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup> The written work of Archimedes has not survived as well as that of <a href="/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">Euclid</a>, and seven of his treatises are known to have existed only through references made to them by other authors. <a href="/wiki/Pappus_of_Alexandria" title="Pappus of Alexandria">Pappus of Alexandria</a> mentions <i>On Sphere-Making</i> and another work on <a href="/wiki/Polyhedron" title="Polyhedron">polyhedra</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Theon_of_Alexandria" title="Theon of Alexandria">Theon of Alexandria</a> quotes a remark about <a href="/wiki/Refraction" title="Refraction">refraction</a> from the <span class="nowrap">now-lost</span> <i>Catoptrica</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[b]</a></sup>
</p><p>Archimedes made his work known through correspondence with the mathematicians in <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>. The writings of Archimedes were first collected by the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine</a> Greek architect <a href="/wiki/Isidore_of_Miletus" title="Isidore of Miletus">Isidore of Miletus</a> (c. 530 AD), while commentaries on the works of Archimedes written by <a href="/wiki/Eutocius_of_Ascalon" title="Eutocius of Ascalon">Eutocius</a> in the sixth century AD helped to bring his work a wider audience. Archimedes' work was translated into Arabic by <a href="/wiki/Th%C4%81bit_ibn_Qurra" title="Thābit ibn Qurra">Thābit ibn Qurra</a> (836–901 AD), and into Latin via Arabic by <a href="/wiki/Gerard_of_Cremona" title="Gerard of Cremona">Gerard of Cremona</a> (c. 1114–1187). Direct Greek to Latin translations were later done by <a href="/wiki/William_of_Moerbeke" title="William of Moerbeke">William of Moerbeke</a> (c. 1215–1286) and <a href="/wiki/Iacopo_da_San_Cassiano" title="Iacopo da San Cassiano">Iacobus Cremonensis</a> (c. 1400–1453).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_science_in_the_Renaissance" title="History of science in the Renaissance">Renaissance</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Editio_princeps" title="Editio princeps">Editio princeps</a></i> (First Edition) was published in <a href="/wiki/Basel" title="Basel">Basel</a> in 1544 by Johann Herwagen with the works of Archimedes in Greek and Latin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surviving_works">Surviving works</span></h3>
<p>The following are ordered chronologically based on new terminological and historical criteria set by Knorr (1978) and Sato (1986).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Measurement_of_a_Circle"><i>Measurement of a Circle</i></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Measurement_of_a_Circle" title="Measurement of a Circle">Measurement of a Circle</a></div>
<p>This is a short work consisting of three propositions. It is written in the form of a correspondence with Dositheus of Pelusium, who was a student of <a href="/wiki/Conon_of_Samos" title="Conon of Samos">Conon of Samos</a>. In Proposition II, Archimedes gives an <a href="/wiki/Approximations_of_%CF%80" title="Approximations of π">approximation</a> of the value of pi (<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span>), showing that it is greater than <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">223</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">71</span></span> and less than <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">22</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">7</span></span>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Sand_Reckoner"><i>The Sand Reckoner</i></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Sand_Reckoner" title="The Sand Reckoner">The Sand Reckoner</a></div>
<p>In this treatise, also known as <i><b>Psammites</b></i>, Archimedes counts the number of <a href="/wiki/Sand" title="Sand">grains of sand</a> that will fit inside the universe. This book mentions the <a href="/wiki/Heliocentrism" title="Heliocentrism">heliocentric</a> theory of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">solar system</a> proposed by <a href="/wiki/Aristarchus_of_Samos" title="Aristarchus of Samos">Aristarchus of Samos</a>, as well as contemporary ideas about the size of the Earth and the distance between various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celestial_bodies" title="Celestial bodies">celestial bodies</a>. By using a system of numbers based on powers of the <a href="/wiki/Myriad" title="Myriad">myriad</a>, Archimedes concludes that the number of grains of sand required to fill the universe is 8<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7001630000000000000♠"></span>63</span></sup> in modern notation. The introductory letter states that Archimedes' father was an astronomer named Phidias. <i>The Sand Reckoner</i> is the only surviving work in which Archimedes discusses his views on astronomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="On_the_Equilibrium_of_Planes"><i>On the Equilibrium of Planes</i></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/On_the_Equilibrium_of_Planes" title="On the Equilibrium of Planes">On the Equilibrium of Planes</a></div>
<p>There are two books to <i>On the Equilibrium of Planes</i>: the first contains seven <a href="/wiki/Axiom" title="Axiom">postulates</a> and fifteen <a href="/wiki/Proposition" title="Proposition">propositions</a>, while the second book contains ten propositions. In the first work, Archimedes proves the <i><a href="/wiki/Torque" title="Torque">Law of the lever</a></i>, which states that:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p><a href="/wiki/Magnitude_(mathematics)" title="Magnitude (mathematics)">Magnitudes</a> are in equilibrium at distances reciprocally proportional to their weights.</p></blockquote>
<p>Archimedes uses the principles derived to calculate the areas and <a href="/wiki/Center_of_mass" title="Center of mass">centers of gravity</a> of various geometric figures including <a href="/wiki/Triangle" title="Triangle">triangles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parallelogram" title="Parallelogram">parallelograms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parabola" title="Parabola">parabolas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-works_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-works-80">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Quadrature_of_the_Parabola"><i>Quadrature of the Parabola</i></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Quadrature_of_the_Parabola" title="Quadrature of the Parabola">Quadrature of the Parabola</a></div>
<p>In this work of 24 propositions addressed to Dositheus, Archimedes proves by two methods that the area enclosed by a <a href="/wiki/Parabola" title="Parabola">parabola</a> and a straight line is 4/3 times the area of a <a href="/wiki/Triangle" title="Triangle">triangle</a> with equal base and height. He achieves this by calculating the value of a <a href="/wiki/Geometric_series" title="Geometric series">geometric series</a> that sums to infinity with the <a href="/wiki/Ratio" title="Ratio">ratio</a> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">4</span></span>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="On_the_Sphere_and_Cylinder"><i>On the Sphere and Cylinder</i></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/On_the_Sphere_and_Cylinder" title="On the Sphere and Cylinder">On the Sphere and Cylinder</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Esfera_Arqu%C3%ADmedes.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1067" data-file-width="1019" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Esfera_Arqu%C3%ADmedes.svg/180px-Esfera_Arqu%C3%ADmedes.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Esfera_Arqu%C3%ADmedes.svg/270px-Esfera_Arqu%C3%ADmedes.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Esfera_Arqu%C3%ADmedes.svg/360px-Esfera_Arqu%C3%ADmedes.svg.png 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Esfera_Arqu%C3%ADmedes.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sphere has 2/3 the volume and surface area of its circumscribing cylinder including its bases.</div></div></div>
<p>In this two-volume treatise addressed to Dositheus, Archimedes obtains the result of which he was most proud, namely the relationship between a <a href="/wiki/Sphere" title="Sphere">sphere</a> and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circumscribe" title="Circumscribe">circumscribed</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cylinder_(geometry)" title="Cylinder (geometry)">cylinder</a> of the same height and <a href="/wiki/Diameter" title="Diameter">diameter</a>. The volume is <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">4</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">3</span></span><span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span><span class="texhtml"><i>r</i></span><sup>3</sup> for the sphere, and 2<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span><span class="texhtml"><i>r</i></span><sup>3</sup> for the cylinder. The surface area is 4<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span><span class="texhtml"><i>r</i></span><sup>2</sup> for the sphere, and 6<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">π</span><span class="texhtml"><i>r</i></span><sup>2</sup> for the cylinder (including its two bases), where <span class="texhtml"><i>r</i></span> is the radius of the sphere and cylinder. The sphere has a volume <span class="nowrap">two-thirds</span> that of the circumscribed cylinder. Similarly, the sphere has an area <span class="nowrap">two-thirds</span> that of the cylinder (including the bases).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="On_Spirals"><i>On Spirals</i></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/On_Spirals" title="On Spirals">On Spirals</a></div>
<p>This work of 28 propositions is also addressed to Dositheus. The treatise defines what is now called the <a href="/wiki/Archimedean_spiral" title="Archimedean spiral">Archimedean spiral</a>. It is the <a href="/wiki/Locus_(mathematics)" title="Locus (mathematics)">locus</a> of points corresponding to the locations over time of a point moving away from a fixed point with a constant speed along a line which rotates with constant <a href="/wiki/Angular_velocity" title="Angular velocity">angular velocity</a>. Equivalently, in <a href="/wiki/Polar_coordinate_system" title="Polar coordinate system">polar coordinates</a> (<span class="texhtml"><i>r</i></span>, <span class="texhtml">θ</span>) it can be described by the equation <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \,r=a+b\theta }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
<mi>r</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>a</mi>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>b</mi>
<mi>θ<!-- θ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \,r=a+b\theta }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\, r=a+b\theta" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d9168c1d8cb658b99ff96f53fae23efcb2a60a6b" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:10.692ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span>  with <a href="/wiki/Real_number" title="Real number">real numbers</a> <span class="texhtml">a</span> and <span class="texhtml">b</span>.
</p><p>This is an early example of a <a href="/wiki/Curve" title="Curve">mechanical curve</a> (a curve traced by a moving <a href="/wiki/Point_(geometry)" title="Point (geometry)">point</a>) considered by a Greek mathematician.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="On_Conoids_and_Spheroids"><i>On Conoids and Spheroids</i></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/On_Conoids_and_Spheroids" title="On Conoids and Spheroids">On Conoids and Spheroids</a></div>
<p>This is a work in 32 propositions addressed to Dositheus. In this treatise Archimedes calculates the areas and volumes of <a href="/wiki/Cross_section_(geometry)" title="Cross section (geometry)">sections</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cone_(geometry)" title="Cone (geometry)">cones</a>, spheres, and paraboloids.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="On_Floating_Bodies"><i>On Floating Bodies</i></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/On_Floating_Bodies" title="On Floating Bodies">On Floating Bodies</a></div><p>
In the first part of this two-volume treatise, Archimedes spells out the law of <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/equilibrium" title="wikt:equilibrium">equilibrium</a> of fluids and proves that water will adopt a spherical form around a center of gravity. This may have been an attempt at explaining the theory of contemporary Greek astronomers such as <a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes" title="Eratosthenes">Eratosthenes</a> that the Earth is round. The fluids described by Archimedes are not <span class="nowrap">self-gravitating</span> since he assumes the existence of a point towards which all things fall in order to derive the spherical shape. Archimedes' <a href="/wiki/Buoyancy" title="Buoyancy">principle of buoyancy</a> is given in this work, stated as follows:</p><blockquote><p>Any body wholly or partially immersed in fluid experiences an upthrust equal to, but opposite in sense to, the weight of the fluid displaced.</p></blockquote>
<p>In the second part, he calculates the equilibrium positions of sections of paraboloids. This was probably an idealization of the shapes of ships' hulls. Some of his sections float with the base under water and the summit above water, similar to the way that icebergs float.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ostomachion"><i>Ostomachion</i></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ostomachion" title="Ostomachion">Ostomachion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stomachion.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="263" data-file-width="270" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Stomachion.JPG/180px-Stomachion.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/21/Stomachion.JPG 1.5x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stomachion.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Ostomachion" title="Ostomachion">Ostomachion</a></i> is a <a href="/wiki/Dissection_puzzle" title="Dissection puzzle">dissection puzzle</a> found in the <a href="/wiki/Archimedes_Palimpsest" title="Archimedes Palimpsest">Archimedes Palimpsest</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Also known as <b>Loculus of Archimedes</b> or <b>Archimedes' Box</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-81">[79]</a></sup> this is a <a href="/wiki/Dissection_puzzle" title="Dissection puzzle">dissection puzzle</a> similar to a <a href="/wiki/Tangram" title="Tangram">Tangram</a>, and the treatise describing it was found in more complete form in the <i><a href="/wiki/Archimedes_Palimpsest" title="Archimedes Palimpsest">Archimedes Palimpsest</a></i>. Archimedes calculates the areas of the 14 pieces which can be assembled to form a <a href="/wiki/Square" title="Square">square</a>. <a href="/wiki/Reviel_Netz" title="Reviel Netz">Reviel Netz</a> of <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a> argued in 2003 that Archimedes was attempting to determine how many ways the pieces could be assembled into the shape of a square. Netz calculates that the pieces can be made into a square 17,152 ways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup> The number of arrangements is 536 when solutions that are equivalent by rotation and reflection are excluded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup> The puzzle represents an example of an early problem in <a href="/wiki/Combinatorics" title="Combinatorics">combinatorics</a>.
</p><p>The origin of the puzzle's name is unclear, and it has been suggested that it is taken from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> word for "throat" or "gullet", <i>stomachos</i> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">στόμαχος</span></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ausonius" title="Ausonius">Ausonius</a> calls the puzzle <span title="Ancient Greek-language text"><i lang="grc">Ostomachion</i></span>, a Greek compound word formed from the roots of <span title="Ancient Greek-language text"><i lang="grc">osteon</i></span> (<span title="Ancient Greek-language text"><span lang="grc">ὀστέον</span></span>, 'bone') and <span title="Ancient Greek-language text"><i lang="grc">machē</i></span> (<span title="Ancient Greek-language text"><span lang="grc">μάχη</span></span>, 'fight').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-81">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_cattle_problem">The cattle problem</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Archimedes%27s_cattle_problem" title="Archimedes's cattle problem">Archimedes' cattle problem</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gotthold_Ephraim_Lessing" title="Gotthold Ephraim Lessing">Gotthold Ephraim Lessing</a> discovered this work in a Greek manuscript consisting of a 44-line poem in the <a href="/wiki/Herzog_August_Library" title="Herzog August Library">Herzog August Library</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wolfenb%C3%BCttel" title="Wolfenbüttel">Wolfenbüttel</a>, Germany in 1773. It is addressed to Eratosthenes and the mathematicians in Alexandria. Archimedes challenges them to count the numbers of cattle in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Cattle_of_Helios" title="The Cattle of Helios">Herd of the Sun</a> by solving a number of simultaneous <a href="/wiki/Diophantine_equation" title="Diophantine equation">Diophantine equations</a>. There is a more difficult version of the problem in which some of the answers are required to be <a href="/wiki/Square_number" title="Square number">square numbers</a>. A. Amthor first solved this version of the problem<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup> in 1880, and the answer is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Very_large_number" title="Very large number">very large number</a>, approximately 7.760271<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7005206544000000000♠"></span>206<span style="margin-left:.25em;">544</span></span></sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Method_of_Mechanical_Theorems"><i>The Method of Mechanical Theorems</i></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Method_of_Mechanical_Theorems" title="The Method of Mechanical Theorems">The Method of Mechanical Theorems</a></div>
<p>This treatise was thought lost until the discovery of the <a href="/wiki/Archimedes_Palimpsest" title="Archimedes Palimpsest">Archimedes Palimpsest</a> in 1906. In this work Archimedes uses <a href="/wiki/Cavalieri%27s_principle" title="Cavalieri's principle">indivisibles</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-:9-7">[7]</a></sup> and shows how breaking up a figure into an infinite number of infinitely small parts can be used to determine its area or volume. He may have considered this method lacking in formal rigor, so he also used the <a href="/wiki/Method_of_exhaustion" title="Method of exhaustion">method of exhaustion</a> to derive the results. As with <i><a href="/wiki/Archimedes%27s_cattle_problem" title="Archimedes's cattle problem">The Cattle Problem</a></i>, <i>The Method of Mechanical Theorems</i> was written in the form of a letter to <a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes" title="Eratosthenes">Eratosthenes</a> in <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Apocryphal_works">Apocryphal works</span></h3>
<p>Archimedes' <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Lemmas" title="Book of Lemmas">Book of Lemmas</a></i> or <i>Liber Assumptorum</i> is a treatise with 15 propositions on the nature of circles. The earliest known copy of the text is in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T._L._Heath" title="T. L. Heath">T. L. Heath</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Clagett" title="Marshall Clagett">Marshall Clagett</a> argued that it cannot have been written by Archimedes in its current form, since it quotes Archimedes, suggesting modification by another author. The <i>Lemmas</i> may be based on an earlier work by Archimedes that is now lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>It has also been claimed that the <a href="/wiki/Heron%27s_formula" title="Heron's formula">formula</a> for calculating the area of a triangle from the length of its sides was known to Archimedes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[c]</a></sup> though its first appearance is in the work of <a href="/wiki/Hero_of_Alexandria" title="Hero of Alexandria">Heron of Alexandria</a> in the 1st century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[86]</a></sup> Other questionable attributions to Archimedes' work include the Latin poem <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carmen_de_ponderibus_et_mensuris" title="Carmen de ponderibus et mensuris">Carmen de ponderibus et mensuris</a></i> (4th or 5th century), which describes the use of a hydrostatic balance to solve the problem of the crown, and the 12th-century text <i><a href="/wiki/Mappae_clavicula" title="Mappae clavicula">Mappae clavicula</a></i>, which contains instructions on how to perform <a href="/wiki/Assay" title="Assay">assaying</a> of metals by calculating their specific gravities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kingcrown_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-kingcrown-90">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Archimedes_Palimpsest">Archimedes Palimpsest</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Archimedes_Palimpsest" title="Archimedes Palimpsest">Archimedes Palimpsest</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Archimedes_Palimpsest.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="448" data-file-width="665" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Archimedes_Palimpsest.jpg/220px-Archimedes_Palimpsest.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Archimedes_Palimpsest.jpg/330px-Archimedes_Palimpsest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Archimedes_Palimpsest.jpg/440px-Archimedes_Palimpsest.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Archimedes_Palimpsest.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In 1906, the Archimedes Palimpsest revealed works by Archimedes thought to have been lost.</div></div></div>
<p>The foremost document containing Archimedes' work is the Archimedes Palimpsest. In 1906, the Danish professor <a href="/wiki/Johan_Ludvig_Heiberg_(historian)" title="Johan Ludvig Heiberg (historian)">Johan Ludvig Heiberg</a> visited <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> to examined a 174-page <a href="/wiki/Goatskin_(material)" title="Goatskin (material)">goatskin</a> <a href="/wiki/Parchment" title="Parchment">parchment</a> of prayers, written in the 13th century, after reading a short transcription published seven years earlier by <a href="/wiki/Athanasios_Papadopoulos-Kerameus" title="Athanasios Papadopoulos-Kerameus">Papadopoulos-Kerameus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-:8-92">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[90]</a></sup> He confirmed that it was indeed a <a href="/wiki/Palimpsest" title="Palimpsest">palimpsest</a>, a document with text that had been written over an erased older work. Palimpsests were created by scraping the ink from existing works and reusing them, a common practice in the Middle Ages, as <a href="/wiki/Vellum" title="Vellum">vellum</a> was expensive. The older works in the palimpsest were identified by scholars as 10th-century copies of previously lost treatises by Archimedes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-:8-92">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[91]</a></sup> The parchment spent hundreds of years in a monastery library in Constantinople before being sold to a private collector in the 1920s. On 29 October 1998, it was sold at auction to an anonymous buyer for $2 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>The palimpsest holds seven treatises, including the only surviving copy of <i>On Floating Bodies</i> in the original Greek. It is the only known source of <i>The Method of Mechanical Theorems</i>, referred to by <a href="/wiki/Suda" title="Suda">Suidas</a> and thought to have been lost forever. <i>Stomachion</i> was also discovered in the palimpsest, with a more complete analysis of the puzzle than had been found in previous texts. The palimpsest was stored at the <a href="/wiki/Walters_Art_Museum" title="Walters Art Museum">Walters Art Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Baltimore" title="Baltimore">Baltimore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a>, where it was subjected to a range of modern tests including the use of <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> and <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a></span> <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a> to read the overwritten text.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[93]</a></sup> It has since returned to its anonymous owner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>The treatises in the Archimedes Palimpsest include:
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Equilibrium_of_Planes" title="On the Equilibrium of Planes">On the Equilibrium of Planes</a></i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/On_Spirals" title="On Spirals">On Spirals</a></i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Measurement_of_a_Circle" title="Measurement of a Circle">Measurement of a Circle</a></i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Sphere_and_Cylinder" title="On the Sphere and Cylinder">On the Sphere and Cylinder</a></i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/On_Floating_Bodies" title="On Floating Bodies">On Floating Bodies</a></i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Method_of_Mechanical_Theorems" title="The Method of Mechanical Theorems">The Method of Mechanical Theorems</a></i></li>
<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stomachion" title="Stomachion">Stomachion</a></i></li>
<li>Speeches by the 4th century BC politician <a href="/wiki/Hypereides" title="Hypereides">Hypereides</a></li>
<li>A commentary on <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Categories_(Aristotle)" title="Categories (Aristotle)">Categories</a></i></li>
<li>Other works</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_things_named_after_Archimedes" title="List of things named after Archimedes">List of things named after Archimedes</a> and <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Eureka_(disambiguation)" title="Eureka (disambiguation)">Eureka</a></div>
<p>Sometimes called the father of mathematics and <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_physics" title="Mathematical physics">mathematical physics</a>, Archimedes had a wide influence on mathematics and science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LitList2_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-LitList2-99">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematics_and_physics">Mathematics and physics</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FieldsMedalFront.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2398" data-file-width="2493" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/FieldsMedalFront.jpg/220px-FieldsMedalFront.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/FieldsMedalFront.jpg/330px-FieldsMedalFront.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/FieldsMedalFront.jpg/440px-FieldsMedalFront.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FieldsMedalFront.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Fields_Medal" title="Fields Medal">Fields Medal</a> carries a portrait of Archimedes.</div></div></div>
<p>Historians of science and mathematics almost universally agree that Archimedes was the finest mathematician from antiquity. <a href="/wiki/Eric_Temple_Bell" title="Eric Temple Bell">Eric Temple Bell</a>, for instance, wrote: 
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Any list of the three “greatest” mathematicians of all history would include the name of Archimedes. The other two usually associated with him are <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Newton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss" title="Carl Friedrich Gauss">Gauss</a>. Some, considering the relative wealth—or poverty—of mathematics and physical science in the respective ages in which these giants lived, and estimating their achievements against the background of their times, would put Archimedes first.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[97]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Likewise, <a href="/wiki/Alfred_North_Whitehead" title="Alfred North Whitehead">Alfred North Whitehead</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_F._Simmons" title="George F. Simmons">George F. Simmons</a>  said of Archimedes:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>... in the year 1500 Europe knew less than Archimedes who died in the year 212 BC ...<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[98]</a></sup></p></blockquote><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>If we consider what all other men accomplished in mathematics and physics, on every continent and in every civilization, from the beginning of time down to the seventeenth century in Western Europe, the achievements of Archimedes outweighs it all. He was a great civilization all by himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[99]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Reviel_Netz" title="Reviel Netz">Reviel Netz</a>, Suppes Professor in Greek Mathematics and Astronomy at <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a> and an expert in Archimedes notes:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>And so, since Archimedes led more than anyone else to the formation of the calculus and since he was the pioneer of the application of mathematics to the physical world, it turns out that Western science is but a series of footnotes to Archimedes. Thus, it turns out that Archimedes is the most important scientist who ever lived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[100]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a> repeatedly expressed admiration for Archimedes, and attributed his invention <a href="/wiki/Architonnerre" title="Architonnerre">Architonnerre</a> to Archimedes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson_Examiner_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson_Examiner-104">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PennyM_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-PennyM-105">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thurston1996_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thurston1996-106">[103]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo</a> called him "superhuman" and "my master",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[105]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Huygens</a> said, "I think Archimedes is comparable to no one" and modeled his work after him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[106]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz">Leibniz</a> said, "He who understands Archimedes and <a href="/wiki/Apollonius_of_Perga" title="Apollonius of Perga">Apollonius</a> will admire less the achievements of the foremost men of later times."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[107]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss" title="Carl Friedrich Gauss">Gauss's</a> heroes were Archimedes and Newton,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[108]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Moritz_Cantor" title="Moritz Cantor">Moritz Cantor</a>, who studied under Gauss in the <a href="/wiki/University_of_G%C3%B6ttingen" title="University of Göttingen">University of Göttingen</a>, reported that he once remarked in conversation that “there had been only three epoch-making mathematicians: Archimedes, <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Newton</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gotthold_Eisenstein" title="Gotthold Eisenstein">Eisenstein</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>The inventor <a href="/wiki/Nikola_Tesla" title="Nikola Tesla">Nikola Tesla</a> praised him, saying:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Archimedes was my ideal. I admired the works of artists, but to my mind, they were only shadows and semblances. The inventor, I thought, gives to the world creations which are palpable, which live and work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[110]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attempts_at_heat_ray_reconstruction">Attempts at heat ray reconstruction</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Archimedes-Mirror_by_Giulio_Parigi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="867" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="119" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Archimedes-Mirror_by_Giulio_Parigi.jpg/220px-Archimedes-Mirror_by_Giulio_Parigi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Archimedes-Mirror_by_Giulio_Parigi.jpg/330px-Archimedes-Mirror_by_Giulio_Parigi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Archimedes-Mirror_by_Giulio_Parigi.jpg/440px-Archimedes-Mirror_by_Giulio_Parigi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Archimedes-Mirror_by_Giulio_Parigi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artistic interpretation of Archimedes' mirror used to burn Roman ships. Painting by <a href="/wiki/Giulio_Parigi" title="Giulio Parigi">Giulio Parigi</a>, c. 1599.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1973 the Greek scientist Ioannis Sakkas carried out a test of the Archimedes heat ray. The experiment took place at the <a href="/wiki/Skaramagas" title="Skaramagas">Skaramagas</a> naval base outside <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>. Seventy mirrors were used, each with a copper coating and a size of around 5 by 3 feet (1.52 m × 0.91 m). The mirrors were pointed at a plywood <span class="nowrap">mock-up</span> of a Roman warship at a distance of around 160 feet (49 m). When the mirrors were focused accurately, the ship burst into flames within a few seconds. The ship had a coating of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bitumen" title="Bitumen">tar</a> paint, which may have aided combustion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[111]</a></sup> Tar coatings were commonplace on ships in the classical era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[d]</a></sup>
</p><p>In October 2005 a group of students from the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">Massachusetts Institute of Technology</a> carried out an experiment with 127 one-foot (30 cm) square mirror tiles, focused on a <span class="nowrap">mock-up</span> wooden ship at a range of around 100 feet (30 m). Flames broke out on a patch of the ship, but only after the sky had been cloudless and the ship had remained stationary for around ten minutes. It was concluded that the device was a feasible weapon under these conditions. The MIT group repeated the experiment for the television show <i><a href="/wiki/MythBusters" title="MythBusters">MythBusters</a></i>, using a wooden fishing boat in <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a> as the target. Again some charring occurred, along with a small amount of flame. To catch fire, wood must reach its <a href="/wiki/Autoignition_temperature" title="Autoignition temperature">autoignition temperature</a>, which is around 300 °C (572 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>When <i>MythBusters</i> broadcast the result of the San Francisco experiment in January 2006, the claim was placed in the category of "busted" (i.e. failed) because of the length of time and the ideal weather conditions required for combustion to occur. It was also pointed out that since Syracuse faces the sea towards the east, the Roman fleet would have had to attack during the morning for optimal gathering of light by the mirrors. <i>MythBusters</i> also pointed out that conventional weaponry, such as flaming arrows or bolts from a catapult, would have been a far easier way of setting a ship on fire at short distances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-death_ray_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-death_ray-118">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2010, <i>MythBusters</i> again looked at the heat ray story in a special edition entitled "<a href="/wiki/MythBusters_(2010_season)#Episode_157_–_&quot;President's_Challenge&quot;" title="MythBusters (2010 season)">President's Challenge</a>". Several experiments were carried out, including a large scale test with 500 schoolchildren aiming mirrors at a <span class="nowrap">mock-up</span> of a Roman sailing ship 400 feet (120 m) away. In all of the experiments, the sail failed to reach the 210 °C (410 °F) required to catch fire, and the verdict was again "busted". The show concluded that a more likely effect of the mirrors would have been blinding, <a href="/wiki/Glare_(vision)" title="Glare (vision)">dazzling</a>, or distracting the crew of the ship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-death_ray2_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-death_ray2-119">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Honors_and_commemorations">Honors and commemorations</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gerhard_Thieme_Archimedes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="717" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Gerhard_Thieme_Archimedes.jpg/220px-Gerhard_Thieme_Archimedes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Gerhard_Thieme_Archimedes.jpg/330px-Gerhard_Thieme_Archimedes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Gerhard_Thieme_Archimedes.jpg/440px-Gerhard_Thieme_Archimedes.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gerhard_Thieme_Archimedes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bronze statue of Archimedes in <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a></div></div></div>
<p>There is a <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">crater</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> named <a href="/wiki/Archimedes_(crater)" title="Archimedes (crater)">Archimedes</a> (<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1073938472">.mw-parser-output .geo-default,.mw-parser-output .geo-dms,.mw-parser-output .geo-dec{display:inline}.mw-parser-output .geo-nondefault,.mw-parser-output .geo-multi-punct{display:none}.mw-parser-output .longitude,.mw-parser-output .latitude{white-space:nowrap}</style><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Archimedes&amp;params=29.7_N_4_W_"><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">29°42′N</span> <span class="longitude">4°00′W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">29.7°N 4.0°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">29.7; -4.0</span></span></span></a></span>) in his honor, as well as a lunar <a href="/wiki/Mountain_range" title="Mountain range">mountain range</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Montes_Archimedes" title="Montes Archimedes">Montes Archimedes</a> (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1073938472" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Archimedes&amp;params=25.3_N_4.6_W_"><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">25°18′N</span> <span class="longitude">4°36′W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">25.3°N 4.6°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">25.3; -4.6</span></span></span></a></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Fields_Medal" title="Fields Medal">Fields Medal</a> for outstanding achievement in mathematics carries a portrait of Archimedes, along with a carving illustrating his proof on the sphere and the cylinder. The inscription around the head of Archimedes is a quote attributed to 1st century AD poet <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Manilius" title="Marcus Manilius">Manilius</a>, which reads in Latin: <i>Transire suum pectus mundoque potiri</i> ("Rise above oneself and grasp the world").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>Archimedes has appeared on postage stamps issued by <a href="/wiki/East_Germany" title="East Germany">East Germany</a> (1973), <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> (1983), <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> (1983), <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a> (1971), <a href="/wiki/San_Marino" title="San Marino">San Marino</a> (1982), and <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> (1963).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>The exclamation of <a href="/wiki/Eureka_(word)" title="Eureka (word)">Eureka!</a> attributed to Archimedes is the state motto of <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>. In this instance, the word refers to the discovery of gold near <a href="/wiki/Sutter%27s_Mill" title="Sutter's Mill">Sutter's Mill</a> in 1848 which sparked the <a href="/wiki/California_Gold_Rush" title="California Gold Rush">California Gold Rush</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[121]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In the preface to <i>On Spirals</i> addressed to Dositheus of Pelusium, Archimedes says that "many years have elapsed since Conon's death." <a href="/wiki/Conon_of_Samos" title="Conon of Samos">Conon of Samos</a> lived c. 280–220 BC, suggesting that Archimedes may have been an older man when writing some of his works.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-73"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-73">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The treatises by Archimedes known to exist only through references in the works of other authors are: <i>On Sphere-Making</i> and a work on <a href="/wiki/Polyhedron" title="Polyhedron">polyhedra</a> mentioned by <a href="/wiki/Pappus_of_Alexandria" title="Pappus of Alexandria">Pappus of Alexandria</a>; <i>Catoptrica</i>, a work on optics mentioned by <a href="/wiki/Theon_of_Alexandria" title="Theon of Alexandria">Theon of Alexandria</a>; <i>Principles</i>, addressed to Zeuxippus and explaining the number system used in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sand_Reckoner" title="The Sand Reckoner">The Sand Reckoner</a></i>; <i>On Balances and Levers</i>; <i>On Centers of Gravity</i>; <i>On the Calendar</i>.</span>

<li id="cite_note-115"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-115">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Lionel_Casson" title="Lionel Casson">Casson, Lionel</a>. 1995. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=sDpMh0gK2OUC&amp;pg=PA18&amp;dq=why+were+homer%27s+ships+black#v=onepage&amp;q=why%20were%20homer's%20ships%20black&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow"><i>Ships and seamanship in the ancient world</i></a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210417145911/https://books.google.com/books?id=sDpMh0gK2OUC&amp;pg=PA18&amp;dq=why+were+homer%27s+ships+black#v=onepage&amp;q=why%20were%20homer's%20ships%20black&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 17 April 2021 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>. Baltimore: <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_University_Press" title="Johns Hopkins University Press">Johns Hopkins University Press</a>. pp. 211–12. <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-8018-5130-8" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-8018-5130-8">978-0-8018-5130-8</a>: "It was usual to smear the seams or even the whole hull with pitch or with pitch and wax". In Νεκρικοὶ Διάλογοι (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dialogues_of_the_Dead" title="Dialogues of the Dead">Dialogues of the Dead</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Lucian" title="Lucian">Lucian</a> refers to coating the seams of a <a href="/wiki/Skiff" title="Skiff">skiff</a> with wax, a reference to pitch (tar) or wax.</span>
</li>
</ol></div>

(1978). <a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2F0315-0860%2878%2990134-9" rel="nofollow">"Archimedes and the spirals: The heuristic background"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Historia_Mathematica" title="Historia Mathematica">Historia Mathematica</a></i>. <b>5</b> (1): 43–75. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2F0315-0860%2878%2990134-9" rel="nofollow">10.1016/0315-0860(78)90134-9</a></span>. <q>"To be sure, Pappus does twice mention the theorem on the tangent to the spiral [IV, 36, 54]. But in both instances the issue is Archimedes' inappropriate use of a 'solid neusis,' that is, of a construction involving the sections of solids, in the solution of a plane problem. Yet Pappus' own resolution of the difficulty [IV, 54] is by his own classification a 'solid' method, as it makes use of conic sections." (p. 48)</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Historia+Mathematica&amp;rft.atitle=Archimedes+and+the+spirals%3A+The+heuristic+background&amp;rft.volume=5&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.pages=43-75&amp;rft.date=1978&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2F0315-0860%2878%2990134-9&amp;rft.aulast=Knorr&amp;rft.aufirst=Wilbur+R.&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fdoi.org%2F10.1016%252F0315-0860%252878%252990134-9&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AArchimedes"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-vitruvius-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-vitruvius_31-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-vitruvius_31-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFVitruvius2006"><a href="/wiki/Vitruvius" title="Vitruvius">Vitruvius</a> (31 December 2006). <a class="external text" href="http://www.gutenberg.org/files/20239/20239-h/20239-h.htm" rel="nofollow"><i><span></span></i>De Architectura<i>, Book IX, paragraphs 9–12</i></a>. <a href="/wiki/Project_Gutenberg" title="Project Gutenberg">Project Gutenberg</a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20191106054452/http://www.gutenberg.org/files/20239/20239-h/20239-h.htm" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 6 November 2019<span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-33">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFRorres">Rorres, Chris (ed.). <a class="external text" href="https://www.math.nyu.edu/~crorres/Archimedes/Crown/Vitruvius.html" rel="nofollow">"The Golden Crown: Sources"</a>. <a href="/wiki/New_York_University" title="New York University">New York University</a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210309151633/https://math.nyu.edu/~crorres/Archimedes/Crown/Vitruvius.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 9 March 2021<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">6 April</span> 2021</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=The+Golden+Crown%3A+Sources&amp;rft.pub=New+York+University&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.math.nyu.edu%2F~crorres%2FArchimedes%2FCrown%2FVitruvius.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AArchimedes"></span>
<div><ul><li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFMorgan1914"><a href="/wiki/Morris_H._Morgan" title="Morris H. Morgan">Morgan, Morris Hicky</a> (1914). <i>Vitruvius: The Ten Books on Architecture</i>. Cambridge: Harvard University Press. pp. 253–254. <q>Finally, filling the vessel again and dropping the crown itself into the same quantity of water, he found that more water ran over the crown than for the mass of gold of the same weight. Hence, reasoning from the fact that more water was lost in the case of the crown than in that of the mass, he detected the mixing of silver with the gold, and made the theft of the contractor perfectly clear.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Vitruvius%3A+The+Ten+Books+on+Architecture&amp;rft.place=Cambridge&amp;rft.pages=253-254&amp;rft.pub=Harvard+University+Press&amp;rft.date=1914&amp;rft.aulast=Morgan&amp;rft.aufirst=Morris+Hicky&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AArchimedes"></span></li><li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFVitruvius1567"><a href="/wiki/Vitruvius" title="Vitruvius">Vitruvius</a> (1567). <i><a href="/wiki/De_architectura" title="De architectura">De Architetura libri decem</a></i>. Venice: Daniele Barbaro. pp. 270–271. <q><i>Postea vero repleto vase in eadem aqua ipsa corona demissa, invenit plus aquae defluxisse in coronam, quàm in auream eodem pondere massam, et ita ex eo, quod plus defluxerat aquae in corona, quàm in massa, ratiocinatus, deprehendit argenti in auro mixtionem, et manifestum furtum redemptoris.</i></q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=De+Architetura+libri+decem&amp;rft.place=Venice&amp;rft.pages=270-271&amp;rft.pub=Daniele+Barbaro&amp;rft.date=1567&amp;rft.au=Vitruvius&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AArchimedes"></span></li></ul></div></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-inaccuracy-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-inaccuracy_34-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFRorres">Rorres, Chris. <a class="external text" href="http://www.math.nyu.edu/~crorres/Archimedes/Crown/CrownIntro.html" rel="nofollow">"The Golden Crown"</a>. <a href="/wiki/Drexel_University" title="Drexel University">Drexel University</a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20090311051318/http://www.math.nyu.edu/~crorres/Archimedes/Crown/CrownIntro.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 11 March 2009<span class="reference-accessdate">. A cylinder or prism C is fixed to a small block that slides freely in the groove (p. 281).</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Journal+for+the+History+of+Astronomy&amp;rft.atitle=The+Material+Culture+of+Greek+Astronomy&amp;rft.volume=30&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.pages=238-307&amp;rft.date=1999-08-01&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1177%2F002182869903000305&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A120800329%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft.issn=0021-8286&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F1999JHA....30..237E&amp;rft.aulast=Evans&amp;rft.aufirst=James&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fdoi.org%2F10.1177%2F002182869903000305&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AArchimedes"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFRusso2013">Russo, L. (2013). <a class="external text" href="https://link.springer.com/content/pdf/10.1007/s40329-013-0016-y.pdf" rel="nofollow">"Archimedes between legend and fact"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <i>Lettera Matematica</i>. <b>1</b> (3): 91–95. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1007%2Fs40329-013-0016-y" rel="nofollow">10.1007/s40329-013-0016-y</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S2CID_(identifier)" title="S2CID (identifier)">S2CID</a> <a class="external text" href="https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:161786723" rel="nofollow">161786723</a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210414104753/https://link.springer.com/content/pdf/10.1007/s40329-013-0016-y.pdf" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span> from the original on 14 April 2021<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">23 March</span> 2021</span>. <q>It is amazing that for a long time Archimedes' attitude towards the applications of science was deduced from the acritical acceptance of the opinion of Plutarch: a polygraph who lived centuries later, in a cultural climate that was completely different, certainly could not have known the intimate thoughts of the scientist. On the other hand, the dedication with which Archimedes developed applications of all kinds is well documented: of catoptrica, as Apuleius tells in the passage already cited (Apologia, 16), of hydrostatics (from the design of clocks to naval engineering: we know from Athenaeus (Deipnosophistae, V, 206d) that the largest ship in Antiquity, the Syracusia, was constructed under his supervision), and of mechanics (from machines to hoist weights to those for raising water and devices of war).</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Lettera+Matematica&amp;rft.atitle=Archimedes+between+legend+and+fact.&amp;rft.volume=1&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.pages=91-95&amp;rft.date=2013&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1007%2Fs40329-013-0016-y&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A161786723%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft.aulast=Russo&amp;rft.aufirst=L.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Flink.springer.com%2Fcontent%2Fpdf%2F10.1007%2Fs40329-013-0016-y.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AArchimedes"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-64"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-64">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFDrachmann1968">Drachmann, A. G. <li id="cite_note-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-65">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation thesis cs1" id="CITEREFCarrier2008">Carrier, Richard (2008). <a class="external text" href="https://clio.columbia.edu/catalog/8602980" rel="nofollow"><i>Attitudes toward the natural philosopher in the early Roman empire (100 B.C. to 313 A.D.)</i></a> (Thesis). <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210414103302/https://clio.columbia.edu/catalog/8602980" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 14 April 2021<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">6 April</span> 2021</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Adissertation&amp;rft.title=Attitudes+toward+the+natural+philosopher+in+the+early+Roman+empire+%28100+B.C.+to+313+A.D.%29&amp;rft.date=2008&amp;rft.aulast=Carrier&amp;rft.aufirst=Richard&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fclio.columbia.edu%2Fcatalog%2F8602980&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AArchimedes"></span> "Hence Plutarch's conclusion that Archimedes disdained all mechanics, shop work, or anything useful as low and vulgar, and only directed himself to geometric theory, is obviously untrue. Thus, as several scholars have now concluded, his account of Archimedes appears to be a complete fabrication, invented to promote the Platonic values it glorifies by attaching them to a much-revered hero." (p.444)</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-121"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-121">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFRiehm2002">Riehm, C. (2002). <a class="external text" href="https://www.ams.org/notices/200207/comm-riehm.pdf" rel="nofollow">"The early history of the Fields Medal"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <i>Notices of the AMS</i>. <b>49</b> (7): 778–782. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210118194529/http://www.ams.org/notices/200207/comm-riehm.pdf" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span> from the original on 18 January 2021<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">28 April</span> 2021</span>. <q>The Latin inscription from the Roman poet Manilius surrounding the image may be translated 'To pass beyond your understanding and make yourself master of the universe.' The phrase comes from Manilius's Astronomica 4.392 from the first century A.D. (p. 782).</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Notices+of+the+AMS&amp;rft.atitle=The+early+history+of+the+Fields+Medal&amp;rft.volume=49&amp;rft.issue=7&amp;rft.pages=778-782&amp;rft.date=2002&amp;rft.aulast=Riehm&amp;rft.aufirst=C.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.ams.org%2Fnotices%2F200207%2Fcomm-riehm.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AArchimedes"></span></span>
</li>
